data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cathode+Materials&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cathode%20materials">cathode materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Disordered+Interlayer&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_disordered%20interlayer">disordered interlayer</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Phase+Transition&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_phase%20transition">phase transition</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cycle+Lifespan&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cycle%20lifespan">cycle lifespan</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as a promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) due to the abundance and low cost of sodium resources compared to lithium. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1−4)</a> The development of efficient SIBs can significantly contribute to energy sustainability, especially in large-scale applications such as grid storage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 5 and 6">(5,6)</a> In general, the construction of high-performance SIBs significantly lies in the design of high rate capability and cyclic reversible cathode materials. While in this field, various candidates have been extensively investigated, including transition-metal oxides, polyanionic frameworks, Prussian blue analogues, and organic compounds. Among them, layered sodium transition-metal oxides (Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>, 0 &lt; <i>x</i> ≤ 1) are particularly attractive owing to their high theoretical capacity, flexible composition, and efficient intercalation mechanism. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Manganese (Mn)-based P2 (P: prismatic)-type materials is one of the most representative materials; however, their development faces significant challenges, particularly resulted from the irreversible phase transition and structural collapse during long-term cycling. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> The Jahn–Teller distortion associated with manganese and the phase transition from P2 to OP4 (O: octahedral) during the dynamic (de)-sodiation process negatively impact their electrochemical performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 9 to 16">(9−16)</a> In recent years, extensive research has been conducted to design the stable Mn-based P2-type cathodes. Various strategies, such as doping with other pillar ions and optimizing the synthesis process, have been employed to enhance the cyclic stability and storage capability. For the previously reported Na<sub>0.7</sub>Li<sub>0.03</sub>[Mg<sub>0.15</sub>Li<sub>0.07</sub>Mn<sub>0.75</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, Li<sup>+</sup> ions occupy two sites within the transition metal (TM) and alkali metal layers, leading to near-zero strain characteristics across a broad voltage range. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> Similarly, in Na<sub>0.75</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>Li<sub>0.15</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Li<sup>+</sup> ions occupy the TM sites and calcium (Ca) ions occupy the alkali metal sites, thus preventing negative phase shifts across a broad voltage range. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> Usually, the introduction of appropriate dopants enables fast ion diffusion under high-volume conditions with limited cycles or minimizes plate sliding under long-cycle conditions with medium volume.</div><div class="NLM_p">At present, controlling structural ordering, specifically the engineering of disordered structures in layered cathodes, is emerging as one of the cutting-edge research directions in this field. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 19 to 22">(19−22)</a> Solids often exhibit varying degrees of disorder, which can lead to unexpected ion or electron conductivity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a> Disorder can be generated through reactions and/or material synthesis. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 25 to 27">(25−27)</a> In a layered cathode, the extraction of base ions causes alkali ion vacancies and TM oxidation, thermodynamically driving the planar rearrangement of the remaining base ions and vacancies, subsequently inducing atomic or electronic phase transitions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> Then the electrostatic interactions are also a key parameter controlling the crystal structure and are often correlated with size effects. For example, adding crystal water to the layered structure of manganese dioxide enlarges the interlayer space. The enlargement occurs as crystal water screens electrostatic interactions between Mg<sup>2+</sup> ions and host anions, thereby promoting alkali-ion diffusion by enhancing local pathways through induced disorder. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 29 to 31">(29−31)</a> As a typical representative, Wang et al. introduced the short-range disorder to disrupt the typical ordered lattice, thereby modifying TM and oxygen interactions, improving the electronic conductivity and electrochemical performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Similarly, another research also highlighted the optimized Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering alongside transition-metal honeycomb ordering to enhance the stability of layered sodium oxide cathode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> To develop high-performance SIBs, it is essential to explore advanced transition-metal-layered oxide cathode materials that combine structural stability with fast ion dynamics.</div><div class="NLM_p">Unlike previous methods, which typically involve high-energy ball milling to mix calcium and sodium sources followed by direct high-temperature calcination, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref34" aria-label="reference 18 and 34">(18,34)</a> this report presents a unique step-by-step molecule–ion exchange method for designing sodium oxide cathodes with expansive and disordered interlayer microenvironment, enabling fast ion mobility and high structural stability. As depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>, Na<sub>0.7</sub>MnO<sub>2.05</sub> (NMO) first forms a hydrated phase through a water-mediated process. During this process, interlayer crystal water often coordinates with Na<sup>+</sup> through electrostatic interaction, increasing the interlayer space and leading to a disordered arrangement of hydrated cations. Upon dehydration, the removal of the bound water facilitates the intercalation of Ca ions. Finally, the ion exchange between Ca<sup>2+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> alters the composition of the layers and the arrangement of atoms, thus affecting the charge balance. The disordered interlayer microenvironment promotes faster diffusion of ions by expanding the interlayer spacing and introducing vacancies through ion exchange. Systematic experimental and density functional theory (DFT) calculation studies indicate that in the alkali metal layer, the presence of Ca<sup>2+</sup> can prevent Mn atoms from migrating into this layer, thereby strengthening the stability of the interlayer. In addition, since the Ca–O interaction generated by Ca<sup>2+</sup> is stronger than the Na–O interaction, this effectively enhances the moisture-resistance of the host in the air environment. Electrochemical performance tests indicate that the optimized Ca<sub>0.065</sub>Na<sub>0.55</sub>MnO<sub>2.05</sub> (CNMO-1) cathode exhibits a high specific capacity of 140.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, with an excellent rate capability (81.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>) and cycle lifespan (2000 cycles). Beyond that, this cathode also exhibits enhanced temperature tolerance yet retains excellent cyclic stability. In detail, it shows a specific capacity of 129.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> and 60 °C, followed by a capacity retention of 89.5% after 400 cycles. When the temperature further improves up to 80 °C, such a cathode can deliver a high specific capacity of 159.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> and a capacity retention of 85.8% after 150 cycles.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/medium/nn4c17035_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic illustration of the preparation of disordered CNMO cathode material via a step-by-step molecule–ion exchange approach.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.4c17035&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a shows the X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of NMO, the dehydration phase after water removal (W-NMO), CNMO-1 and CNMO-1.5, which exchanged Na<sup>+</sup> and Ca<sup>2+</sup> ions after water-mediated treatment. All samples exhibit similar diffraction peaks, which closely match the standard card (JCPDS No. 27-0751). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>) shows the hydration phase formed by stirring water and NMO stirred directly with a solution of CaCl<sub>2</sub> (NCMO-1). Excess alkali introduced during the preparation process led to the formation of residual alkali on the surface of synthesized Na<sub>0.7</sub>MnO<sub>2.05</sub>. To confirm the removal of H<sub>2</sub>O and NaHCO<sub>3</sub> impurities from CNMO-1, multiple characterization methods were employed. The absence of the O–H stretching signal at 2500–3500 cm<sup>–1</sup> and the absence of NaHCO<sub>3</sub> peaks at 1000–1500 cm<sup>–1</sup> in the Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectrum of CNMO-1 indicate the elimination of water molecules and NaHCO<sub>3</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). According to thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) results, no mass loss is observed between 30 and 300 °C in CNMO-1, corroborating the removal of H<sub>2</sub>O (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>), which aligns well with the XRD and FTIR findings. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> Compared to the XRD pattern of pristine NMO, the (002) reflection of W-NMO and CNMO-1 showed asymmetric broadening, with the lattice spacing increasing from 0.555 nm (NMO) to 0.559 nm (W-NMO). Conversely, the lattice spacing of (002) decreased from 0.559 nm (CNMO-1) to 0.557 nm (CNMO-1.5), likely due to Ca ions’ higher charge density, and stronger electrostatic interactions pull the layers closer (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b). Excess Ca<sup>2+</sup> in the alkali metal layer reduces the lattice spacing of (002). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> The ionic radius of Ca<sup>2+</sup> closely matches that of alkali metal ions like Na<sup>+</sup>, making it more compatible with the larger, open spaces in the alkali layer, where it typically adopts prismatic or octahedral coordination with oxygen atoms rather than the more confined TM layer. Simultaneously, the (100) diffraction peak shifts from 36.08° (NMO) to 36.31° (CNMO-1), demonstrating a reduction in the lattice parameter a from 2.864 to 2.837 due to the oxidation of Mn.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/medium/nn4c17035_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Structural characteristics. (a) XRD patterns, (b) enlarged XRD patterns, (c) Rietveld refined XRD pattern with the corresponding cell parameters of the as-synthesized disordered CNMO-1 material, and (d) schematic crystal structure of disordered CNMO-1. (e) HAADF-STEM image of CNMO-1 and the corresponding (f) intensity line profiles obtained from the area marked by white rectangles, and (g) SEM, (h) HRTEM, and (i) Raman spectra. (j) FTIR spectra of pristine NMO and CNMO-1 after being exposed in moist air. Calculation model and the corresponding adsorption energy of Ca (k) and Na (l) to water in NCMO.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.4c17035&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The chemical states of Mn, Ca, and O in the NMO and CNMO-1 cathode materials were analyzed using X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). The average oxidation state of Mn increased from +3.41 in NMO to +3.66 in CNMO-1, indicating a higher proportion of Mn<sup>4+</sup> in the latter. To maintain charge neutrality, the proportion of O<sup>2–</sup> increased from 61.2% to 68.3%, corresponding to the higher Mn-oxidation state and the formation of Ca–O bonds. These changes ensure overall charge balance and enhance the electrochemical stability of CNMO-1 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S4 and S5,</a> <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c shows the XRD Rietveld refinement of the CNMO-1 material. The hexagonal P2 structure with the space group <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> is further confirmed as the calculated profile fits well with the experimental data (<i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> = 1.97%). Detailed structural information, including thermal factors, atomic sites, and occupancies, is provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d schematically illustrates the crystal structure of CNMO-1, where Ca ions occupy prismatic Na_e sites at the edges and Na_f sites at the center of the TM layers. The atomic-level structure of CNMO-1 was precisely characterized by using the high-angle annular dark-field (HAADF) STEM images, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e. The interlayer lattice distance between adjacent TM layers is measured to be 0.278 nm, corresponding to the <i>d</i>-spacing of the (001) planes in the P2-layered structure. The intensity line profile extracted from the STEM image further differentiates between Ca and Na atoms within CNMO-1 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f). Analysis by inductively coupled plasma atomic emission spectroscopy (ICP-AES) revealed an increase in the Ca content and a corresponding decrease in the Na content in the CNMO-1 and CNMO-1.5 samples compared to the W-NMO samples (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2,</a> <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). These compositional changes confirm the successful exchange of Na<sup>+</sup> with Ca<sup>2+</sup> ions within the cathode structure. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>) presents a scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image, showing that CNMO-1 particles are of micrometer size and exhibit a different morphology and size distribution compared to NMO. Energy-dispersive spectrometry (EDS) mappings demonstrate that Na, Mn, Ca, and O elements are uniformly distributed throughout the CNMO-1 and CNMO-1.5 particles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S7</a>, Supporting Information). The layer spacing of ordered NMO is smaller than that of CNMO-1 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>g and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S8</a>, Supporting Information). The transmission electron microscopy (TEM) image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>h) further revealed the crystallographic information on CNMO-1, where the lattice fringes show a layer spacing of 0.559 nm, indexed as the (002) crystal faces of the layered CNMO-1 (JCPDS No. 27-0751). Within the TM layer and the alkali metal layer, electrostatic interactions stabilize the layered stacking arrangement. The hydration phase (Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>·1.5H<sub>2</sub>O) expands the interlayer space by forming hydrogen bonds between water molecules and metal hydroxides, mediated by water, thus promoting the disordered arrangement of the alkali metal layers. Subsequently, Ca ions are exchanged with Na ions, altering the atomic composition and the arrangement of the interlayer. From the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns, the Na disordering in CNMO-1 is captured at the atomic level, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a> (Supporting Information). Referring to the polarized Raman results, the Raman peak at 460 cm<sup>–1</sup> is identified as the E<sub>2g</sub> mode, and the peak at 576 cm<sup>–1</sup> is the A<sub>1g</sub> mode of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> In the as-prepared CNMO-1 and NMO samples, these peaks shift to 550 cm<sup>–1</sup> (E<sub>2g</sub>) and 604 cm<sup>–1</sup> (A<sub>1g</sub>), respectively. Notably, the broadened shoulder peak at 643 cm<sup>–1</sup> in CNMO-1 may indicate Na ion disorder, suggesting structural disorder, as confirmed by refined XRD results. Air stability is essential for the practical use of electrode materials in energy storage devices. Layered sodium TM oxides (Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>) often undergo structural and chemical transformations when exposed to moist air. To monitor the structural changes in NMO and CNMO-1 upon moist air exposure, samples were placed in the air atmosphere (moderate humidity of 60%) at room temperature and XRD data were collected simultaneously (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>, Supporting Information). When NMO was exposed to air for 15 days, a small peak appeared to the left of peak (002). In contrast, no similar phenomenon was observed after 60 days of air exposure in CNMO-1. The FTIR spectra revealed an O–H stretching signal at 2500–3500 cm<sup>–1</sup>, indicating the presence of water molecules (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>j). Notably, a NaHCO<sub>3</sub> peak at 2000–3000 cm<sup>–1</sup> is present in the NMO spectrum but absent in the CNMO-1 spectrum, suggesting that CNMO-1 exhibits superior stability in moist air compared to NMO. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>k,l presents a model of the binding of Na and Ca atoms to water in CNMO-1. Theoretical calculations show that the binding energy of Ca to water is stronger than that of Na, which inhibits the direct binding of water molecules to Na atoms.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a shows the average discharge specific capacities of the ordered NMO, disordered CNMO-1, and disordered CNMO-1.5 cathodes at various current densities. At 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, and 5.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacities of CNMO-1 were 140.4, 135.4, 117.2, 104.7, 86.5, and 73 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. These values were significantly higher than the specific capacities of ordered NMO (113, 104.2, 86.8, 66, 53.8, and 42 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) and disordered CNMO-1.5 (102.1, 93, 82.9, 73.9, 65.8, and 54.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a> (Supporting Information) showed the performance of W-NMO (135, 129, 114.8, 100.9, 87, and 64.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) and NCMO-01 (132.2, 128.5, 109.2, 82.3, 55.5, 27.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). The capacity of CNMO-1.5 is lower than that of CNMO-1 due to excessive Ca<sup>2+</sup> exchanging with Na<sup>+</sup>, where Na occupies less space, resulting in decreased specific capacities. The rate capability of W-NMO is better than that of NMO, indicating that water-mediated treatment expands the interlayer space and facilitates ion diffusion. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a> (Supporting Information) shows the GCD curve of the CNMO-1 cathode at different current densities ranging from 0.1 to 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Notably, at all current densities, the smooth cycle curve shows no obvious electrochemical plateau, unlike the distinct plateau in NMO (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>, Supporting Information). The ultrafast storage performance of disordered CNMO-1 electrodes surpasses that of most previously reported layered oxides (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>, Supporting Information). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref14 ref15 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref14 ref15 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 12, 14, 15 and 37 to 43">(12,14,15,37−43)</a> <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b,c compares the cycle performances of CNMO-1, CNMO-1.5, and NMO at current densities of 0.2 and 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 10 cycles of activation at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. At a current density of 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the CNMO-1 electrode delivered a specific capacity of 118.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 400 cycles with a capacity retention of 87.4%. This surpasses the CNMO-1.5 counterpart, which had a retention of 104.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 78.5%, and the NMO counterpart, which had a retention of 43.68 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 39%. Moreover, the Coulombic efficiencies of CNMO-1 and CNMO-1.5 were 99.14% and 99.22%, respectively, both of which are higher than the NMO’s Coulombic efficiency of 98.34% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15,</a> Supporting Information). In addition, after 15 days in moist air, CNMO-1 retained 92.7% of its capacity after 300 cycles at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16</a> (Supporting Information). At the high current density of 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the CNMO-1 cathode retained a high specific capacity of 75.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 2000 cycles with a capacity retention rate of 89%. This performance was superior to those of NMO (19.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, 28.8%) and CNMO-1.5 (45.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, 72.1%). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17</a> (Supporting Information) shows that the long-term cycling performance of W-NNO and NCMO-1 at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> is significantly lower than that of disordered CNMO-1, underscoring the importance of step-by-step synthesis. Notably, at the higher current density of 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the CNMO-1 cathode retained a high specific capacity of 75.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with a retention rate of 93% after 2000 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18</a>, Supporting Information). According to the GCD diagram, the phenomenon of voltage lag during the cycling process is not obvious (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19</a>, Supporting Information).</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/medium/nn4c17035_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical performance test. (a) Rate capability, (b) long-term cycle performance at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 10 cycles of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> activation and (c) at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Comparison between this work and the previously reported layered oxides as cathodes for SIBs at different current densities. High-temperature performance test of the disordered CNMO-1 and ordered NMO cathodes. (e) Cycle performance at 60 °C and 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 5 cycles of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> and (f) cycle performance at 80 °C and 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 5 cycles of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.4c17035&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a> (Supporting Information) compared the cycling performance of previously reported layered oxides as cathodes at different current densities, highlighting their high capacity retention rates and large specific capacities for both high and low current densities. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref14 ref15 ref18 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref14 ref15 ref18 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 12, 14, 15, 18 and 36 to 40">(12,14,15,18,36−40)</a> Considering that the battery operating in a high-temperature environment will exacerbate cathode instability, leading to complex irreversible structural evolution and weakened performance, it is crucial to explore the high-temperature resistance of the CNMO-1 cathode. At a high temperature of 60 °C, both CNMO-1 and NMO electrodes were activated for 5 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> and then cycling continued at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e. The CNMO-1 electrode maintained a high specific capacity of 116 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 400 cycles. In contrast, the capacity of the NMO electrode was severely attenuated with a specific capacity of only 45.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. Surprisingly, the GCD curve of the CNMO-1 electrode at the 400th cycle still showed relatively obvious platforms, while the GCD curve of the NMO electrode nearly collapsed (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S20</a>, Supporting Information). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f shows that at the higher temperature of 80 °C and a current density of 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the CNMO-1 electrode still maintained a high specific capacity of 137.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 150 cycles, with a retention rate of 85.8%. However, the electrochemical performance of the NMO electrode also declined significantly, maintaining a specific capacity of 47.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with a retention rate of 41.6%.</div><div class="NLM_p">Additionally, the electrochemical reaction behavior and kinetic processes of the cathode were studied using cyclic voltammetry (CV), galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT), electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), and Raman spectra. The CV curves of the CNMO-1 and NMO cathodes at different scanning rates from 0.1 to 5.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup> are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S21</a> (Supporting Information). According to the redox and phase transition mechanisms, the pristine ordered NMO electrode can be divided into three regions: P–O phase transition region, P2-phase region, and Jahn–Teller distortion region (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 44 and 45">(44,45)</a> The P–O phase transition region occurs at 3.6–4.0 V. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> At 2.5–3.6 V, the NMO electrode shows a P2 structure and exhibits fast kinetics and high redox reversibility. The redox peaks at this stage could be attributed to the rearrangement of Na-vacancy-ordering superstructures and Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> orderings. Due to the Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox reaction, the Jahn–Teller distortion occurs at voltages below 2.5 V, which has also been termed as P2–P2′ phase transformation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> Compared with NMO, the contribution to the specific capacity of CNMO-1 by Jahn–Teller distortion regions is reduced. The CNMO-1 electrode shows much smoother CV curves and d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plots (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S22</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that local structural transitions of disordered CNMO-1 are mitigated and the polarization of the positive electrode material has been effectively controlled.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/medium/nn4c17035_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrode process kinetics of CNMO-1 and NMO. (a) Comparison of the <i>CV</i> curve of the NMO and CNMO-1 electrode at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) GITT curves and the calculated diffusion coefficient values. (c) Ex situ Raman spectra of disordered CNMO-1. (d) Schematic diagram of the process of Na<sup>+</sup> rearrangement. (e) DRT evolution of the in situ impedance of disordered CNMO-1.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.4c17035&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Furthermore, to check the capacitive contributions in both ordered NMO and disordered CNMO-1 electrodes, we analyzed the anodic peaks located at ∼2.5 V (Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox peak) and 3.5 V. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-1"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-2"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-3"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-4"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-5">i</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-6"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-7" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-8"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-9">a</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-10"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-11" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">v</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-12" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">b</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-1"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>i</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>a</mi><msup><mi>v</mi><mi>b</mi></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div>where <i>i</i> corresponds to the current densities at various scan rates (mV s<sup>–1</sup>) and <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are adjustable constants. When the <i>b</i> value approaches 0.5 or 1, the redox reactions tend to be diffusion-controlled or surface-controlled, respectively. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S23</a> (Supporting Information), the <i>b</i> values of NMO and CNMO-1 electrodes at 2.5 V are 0.505 and 0.684, respectively, suggesting a diffusion-controlled characteristic for Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox reactions. When the potential increases to 3.5 V (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S24</a>, Supporting Information), the <i>b</i> values increase to 0.908 and 0.952 for NMO and CNMO-1 electrodes. Compared to the NMO electrode, the CNMO-1 electrode exhibits a greater surface faradaic contribution due to its open morphology.<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-13"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-14"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-15"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-16"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-17">i</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-18"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-19" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-20"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-21"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-22" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">k</span><span class="MJXp-mn MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-23" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;">1</span></span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-24">v</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-25" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-26"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-27" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">k</span><span class="MJXp-mn MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-28" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;">2</span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-29"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-30" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">v</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-31" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-32">1</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-33">/</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-34">2</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-2"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>i</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mi>k</mi><mn>1</mn></msub><mi>v</mi><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>k</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><msup><mi>v</mi><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div>where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> represents the current response from capacitive processes, while <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> correspond to the current generated by diffusion processes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S25</a> (Supporting Information), the capacitance contribution ratio of the disordered CNMO-1 cathode was calculated to be 47%, 49%, 54%, 61%, 71%, and 85% at scanning rates of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S26</a> exhibited the representative CV profile at 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> with a 49% pseudocapacitive contribution (orange area) to the total current. To better clarify the reaction kinetics, a GITT test was conducted to determine the sodium-ion diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub>) of ordered NMO and disordered CNMO-1 electrodes. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b presents the calculated <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> values during discharge and charge. In the 2.2–2.5 V range, corresponding to Mn redox, the electrostatic interaction within the Na–O bond increases, inhibiting sodium-ion diffusion. CNMO-1, with its larger layer spacing, experiences weaker electrostatic repulsion between Na–O bond. As a result, <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> for CNMO-1 showed only a slight decrease to 3.16 × 10<sup>–12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, while NMO experiences a more significant drop to 2.34 × 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>. The <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> of both CNMO-1 and NMO remained stable within the 2.5–3.5 V range; however, CNMO-1 exhibited a higher value of 3.16 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> compared to 1.58 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> for NMO. This enhanced ion mobility in CNMO-1 can be attributed to its expanded interlayer spacing, altered alkali layer composition, and increased disorder in the Na layer, all of which contribute to a more efficient diffusion process. At charge potentials above 3.5 V, the <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> of NMO drops sharply to 1.77 × 10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, indicating that the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channels are compromised due to Mn migration to alkali ion sites, which hinders Na ions transport. In contrast, CNMO-1 maintains a high <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> of 9.95 × 10<sup>–12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, suggesting that its ionic pathways remain unaffected throughout the entire charge–discharge process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To demonstrate the change in Na distribution, ex situ Raman spectra of NMO and CNMO-1 were measured from the first to the fifth cycle. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c presents contour plots of these ex situ Raman spectra, where 550 cm<sup>–1</sup> corresponds to E<sub>2g</sub>, 600 cm<sup>–1</sup> to A<sub>1g</sub>, and 640 cm<sup>–1</sup> to Na ions disordered distribution. The contour map clearly shows that CNMO-1 consistently exhibits a prominent peak at 640 cm<sup>–1</sup> throughout the cycling process, indicating a disordered Na ion arrangement. Conversely, the peak at 640 cm<sup>–1</sup> progressively diminishes in NMO during the cycling process, suggesting a trend toward an ordered Na<sup>+</sup> ion arrangement (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S27</a>, Supporting Information). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> The gradual ordering of NMO during cycling is likely driven by the crystal field stabilization energy. As the cathodes charge, alkali ion vacancies emerge in the interlayer spaces, causing oxygen atoms to face each other directly across these gaps. In the high charge state (SOC) of the NMO, the migration of Mn to these vacant alkali ion sites is favored, helping to balance the charge distribution and thus contributing to the transition from a disordered to an ordered state. However, CNMO-1 retains its disordered structure throughout the cycle, with Ca ions stabilizing the alkali metal layer and thus keeping Mn from migrating. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49 ref50" aria-label="reference 49 and 50">(49,50)</a> <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d illustrates the variation in Na distribution in disordered CNMO-1 and ordered NMO materials during the cycling process. To further elucidate the contribution of chemical bonds to structural stability, differential charge density analysis and crystal orbital Hamilton population (COHP) calculations were employed to examine the local charge distributions and bonding characteristics of Ca–O and Na–O bonds. First, differential charge density analysis revealed that larger charge clouds indicate stronger interactions. The calculated binding energies are −4.75 eV for Na–O and −7.37 eV for Ca–O (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S28</a>, Supporting Information), demonstrating that the Ca–O bond is significantly stronger than the Na–O bond. Subsequently, the COHP method, which decomposes the contributions of individual atoms to bonding, nonbonding, or antibonding states, further supports this finding. The COHP value for the Ca–O bond is −0.79, whereas it is −0.55 for the Na–O bond. A more negative COHP value signifies a stronger bonding interaction, resulting in a more rigid and stable structure. Furthermore, to investigate the influence of Ca<sup>2+</sup> ions on the electronic environment of the alkali metal layer and their effect on manganese (Mn) migration, migration energy barriers and vacancy formation energies for Mn are calculated. The migration energy barrier for Mn in CNMO-1 is 1.59 eV, significantly higher than 0.51 eV in NMO (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S29</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that Ca<sup>2+</sup> ions effectively inhibit Mn migration into the Na layer. Additionally, the Mn vacancy configurations presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S30</a> show vacancy formation energies of −0.96 eV for NMO and −0.08 eV for CNMO-1. The reduced vacancy formation energy in CNMO-1 suggests that Ca<sup>2+</sup> enhances structural stability by decreasing the likelihood of Mn vacancies.</div><div class="NLM_p">The influence of the Na ion disordered arrangement and structural stability on electrochemical kinetics was further analyzed by in situ EIS measurement. The Nyquist plots of in situ EIS were collected from 1st to 25th as displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S31</a> (Supporting Information). Various kinetic processes with distinct relaxation features are evident across timescales, with charge transfer in layered oxide cathodes occurring between 10<sup>–1</sup> s and 10<sup>–2</sup> s, while diffusion, potentially the rate-determining step in battery kinetics, has a time constant extending beyond 10 s <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S32</a> (Supporting Information) showed the DRT of the in situ impedance diagram. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>e and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S33</a> show contour plots of DRT from in situ impedance diagrams, revealing relaxation times of <i>t</i> = 101.95 s for Warburg diffusion impedance and <i>t</i> = 10<sup>–0.95</sup> s for charge transfer impedance. To clearly illustrate the impedance changes between ordered NMO and disordered CNMO-1, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S34</a> (Supporting Information) presents the corresponding impedance values in the 1 and 25 cycles. The diffusion impedance in disordered CNMO-1 decreased from 181 to 162 Ω between cycle 1 and cycle 25, while in ordered NMO, it increased significantly from 90.6 to 269 Ω over the same cycles. The Na-disordered structure introduces additional pathways for ion diffusion, which effectively reduces the resistance of CNMO-1. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> Meanwhile, the charge transfer resistance increases from 7.5 to 105 Ω in CNMO-1 and from 5 to 202 Ω in NMO. The higher impedance observed in NMO compared to that in CNMO-1 is primarily attributed to structural degradation of the cathode material, including phase transitions, particle fracture, and Mn migration, which disrupts the efficient transport of electrons and ions, thereby impeding sodium ion diffusion.</div><div class="NLM_p">As the structural stability of cathode materials directly correlates with electrochemical performance, we employed an in situ XRD method to monitor the structural evolution of NMO and CNMO-1 within the voltage range 2–4 V at 0.5 C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a,b). For NMO, significant changes in the position and intensity of the (002) and (004) peaks were observed during the second and third charge–discharge cycles. Additionally, a (004) peak emerged at approximately 17–18° in the high-voltage range of 3.8–4.0 V, indicating the P2-OP4 phase transition. In the low-voltage range of 2–2.2 V, the (102) and (104) peaks gradually disappeared, accompanied by the formation of new (114) and (116) peaks, which can be indexed to a P′2 phase resulting from the Jahn–Teller ionization of Mn<sup>3+</sup>. In contrast, disordered CNMO-1 showed minimal changes in the position and intensity of the (002) peaks, without any new peak signals, indicating the successful suppression of high-voltage P2-OP4 and low voltage P2–P′2 phase transitions. During the four charge–discharge cycles, the variation of such characteristic peaks in CNMO-1 followed the same pattern observed during the first charge. Suppressing phase transitions is essential for maintaining consistent performance and is crucial for the practical application of cathode in battery.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/medium/nn4c17035_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Study on the structural and morphological evolution of ordered NMO and disordered CNMO-1 electrodes upon cycling. Voltage profiles and contour plots of in situ XRD patterns for (a) CNMO-1 and (b) NMO. HRTEM images were recorded in the fifth cycle and charged to 4 V: (c) CNMO-1 and (d) NMO. Partial enlarged HRTEM images of (e) CNMO-1 and (f) NMO. Ex situ FESEM images of (g) CNMO-1 and (h) NMO electrodes after 100 cycles at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.4c17035&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c,d presents the HRTEM images of CNMO-1 and NMO after five cycles, followed by charging to 4.0 V. In CNMO-1, the lattice fringes exhibited layered and point-like defects with noticeable nonuniformity, suggesting potential short-range disorder. In contrast, the lattice fringes of NMO appear more uniformly and systematically arranged. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e,f shows the corresponding local HRTEM images. In NMO, due to the formation of the OP4 phase, the interlayer distance shrinks significantly at 4.0 V. The interlayer distances of ordered NMO were 5.57 and 4.98 Å, respectively. In contrast, the interlayer distances of CNMO-1 at 4.0 V were 5.61 and 5.59 Å, indicating that the Na layer in CNMO-1 does not undergo significant distance shrinkage. This stability can be attributed to the presence of Ca ions, which act as pillar ions and inhibit the formation of the OP4 phase. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S35</a> (Supporting Information) presents the FESEM images of NMO and CNMO-1 electrodes after 50 cycles, while <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g,h, showed the FESEM images of CNMO-1 and NMO electrodes after 100 cycles, respectively. The images revealed that the CNMO-1 electrode retains well-defined, block-like particles with clear edges and relatively smooth surfaces. The particles are uniform in size, and there appears to be minimal agglomeration, suggesting that the material retains its structural integrity after cycling. In contrast, NMO electrodes have a messy structure with layers that look like uneven sheets. The particles are irregular and showed visible folds and overlapping layers, suggesting that the structure has changed a lot and may be breaking down. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S36</a> (Supporting Information) shows the XRD patterns of disordered CNMO-1 and ordered NMO samples after the 50th and 100th cycles. The position and intensity of the XRD peaks for disordered CNMO-1 showed minimal change, indicating that the material’s structural integrity was maintained and degradation was minimal. In contrast, the XRD pattern of ordered NMO exhibited significant changes, particularly a reduction in the intensity of the (002) peak, suggesting a phase transition or structural degradation during cycling.</div><div class="NLM_p">Based on the theory of electrode reaction thermodynamics, the role of Ca ions in maintaining the stable structure of the CNMO-1 high SOC was studied by DFT calculation. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a illustrates the formation energy difference between P2 and OP4 at Na contents of 1, 0.5, and 0.25 for NMO (Na contents of 1, 0.5, and 0.25 correspond to points A, B, and C). In the P2-structured NMO, the oxygen layer stacking order follows a BAAB–BAAB sequence (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b). Within this structure, Na ions are located between two adjacent oxygen layers (B and A or A and B) and are coordinated by six oxygen atoms arranged in a trigonal prism. From the perspective of a single slab, there is insufficient Na in the alkali metal layer to stabilize the P-type configuration, especially at high SOC, directly inducing slab shrinkage and consequent octahedral prismatic-type (OP-type) layer formation. The oxygen layer stacking order in the OP4 structure follows a CAAC-BAAB sequence (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c), which introduces a shift in the oxygen layers, resulting in orthorhombic symmetry and making the structure more complex than the P2 phase. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> NMO and CNMO-1 materials had different crystal structures in the P2 and OP4 phases under different charging states (corresponding to varying Na contents).</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/medium/nn4c17035_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Theoretical investigations on the reaction thermodynamics of electrodes. (a) Simulated the phase formation energy changes of ordered NMO at various desodiation states for both the P2 and OP4 phases. Corresponding crystal structures of (b) P2 and (c) OP4 under different desodiation states. (d) Simulated the phase formation energy changes of CNMO-1 for the P2 and OP4 phases across different Ca content states. Corresponding crystal structures of (e) P2 and (f) OP4 under different Ca content states.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.4c17035&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">It is evident that as the Na content decreases, the energy required to form OP4 consistently decreases. At a Na content of 0.25, the energy required to form OP4 is lower than that for P2, indicating that a structural transformation from P2 to OP4 has occurred. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>d demonstrates the impact of increasing Ca content in CNMO-1 on the formation energy of the OP4 phase at a Na content of 0.25. The data revealed that as Ca content rises, the energy required to form the OP4 phase correspondingly increases. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 6</a>e,f are P2 and OP4 structure models of CNMO corresponding to different Ca<sup>2+</sup> contents. With the increase of Ca content, the formation energy of the OP4 phase exceeds that of the P2 phase, indicating that Ca ions contribute to structural stabilization and effectively prevent the phase transition.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a> shows the mechanism diagram of the design of the air-stability, high-capacity, and long-lifespan disordered CNMO cathode. The interlayer microenvironment is regulated through a step-by-step molecular–ion exchange method. This approach creates expanded spaces and a disordered Na layer, which facilitate rapid ion diffusion. Additionally, the incorporation of Ca ions into the alkali metal layer inhibits Mn migration and phase transitions, as the stronger Ca–O bond interactions, compared to Na–O bonds, help maintain a robust structure. The stepwise synthesis strategy had also been applied to KNMO-1, ZNMO-1, and LNMO-1 materials to verify the exchange of different ions in layered sodium oxides. This successful application demonstrates the broader feasibility of the strategy for various layered sodium oxides. SEM images and corresponding mappings revealed a uniform distribution of the Na, Mn, and O elements throughout these particles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>a–c). These uniform distributions confirm the homogeneous incorporation of these elements within the materials. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 8</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S37</a> (Supporting Information), similar XRD curves, layered structures, and bulk morphology of disordered CNMO-1 are observed for treated KNMO-1, ZNMO-1, and LNMO-1 materials. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>e presents a comparison of the rate performance of the three materials, showing the average discharge-specific capacities of KNMO-1, ZNMO-1, and LNMO-1 cathodes at various current densities. At 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, and 5.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the specific capacities of KNMO-1 were 115, 110, 102.2, 92.2, 79.7, and 54.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, of ZNMO-1 were 113.6, 111.7, 104.9, 94.9, 82.1, and 59.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, and of LNMO-1 were 116.1, 113.5, 104.6, 93.3, 79.3, and 55 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Their rate capability performance showed improvement compared to ordered NMO. At a current density of 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> within 2.0–4.0 V after activation at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for five cycles, the KNMO-1, ZNMO-1, and LNMO-1 electrodes delivered discharge capacities of 69.2, 73, and 59 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at six cycles, respectively, with 87.7%, 93.2%, and 92.7% of the original discharge capacities retained after 1200 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>f). As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S38</a> (Supporting Information), the KNMO-1, ZNMO-1, and LNMO-1 electrodes showed similar charge–discharge curves to CNMO-1, with a smooth slope within 2.0–4.0 V. The comparison of the capacity retention rates for KNMO-1, ZNMO-1, LNMO-1, and NMO over 1200 cycles at a current density of 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> revealed significant differences (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>g). Notably, the capacity retention rate of NMO is 37.3%, which is significantly lower than that of the other three materials, indicating that this strategy significantly improves the cycle performance.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/medium/nn4c17035_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Mechanism diagram of the design of air-stability, high-capacity, and long-lifespan disordered CNMO cathode, which was enabled by interlayer microenvironment regulation.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.4c17035&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/medium/nn4c17035_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0008.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. Extensiveness of the stepwise synthesis method. SEM images and the corresponding mappings of (a) KNMO-1, (b) ZNMO-1, and (c) LNMO-1. (d) XRD patterns. (e) Rate capability. (f) Long-term cycle performance at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 5 cycles of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> activation. (g) Comparison of capacity retention rates for LNMO-1, KNMO-1, ZNMO-1, and NMO over 1200 cycles at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.4c17035&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">This research put forward the design concept of a moisture-resistant, expansive, and disordered interlayer microenvironment to reshape the structural properties of Mn-based P2-type cathode materials and improve the storage performance. The combination of experiment and theoretical calculation revealed a multiple function of such a molecule–ion exchange regulated strategy. On the one hand, water-mediated processes and ion exchange can induce disorder in the sodium layer. This disordered interlayer microenvironment reduced electrostatic interactions between the layers, creating vacancies that provide additional pathways for ion diffusion, thereby facilitating ion diffusion kinetics. On the other hand, the stronger Ca–O bond interaction than that of the Na–O bond case and Ca<sup>2+</sup> prevented Mn migration, indicating their role in maintaining structural stability and interlayer disorder. As electrochemical performance tests indicated, such a disordered CNMO-1 cathode showed superior rate capability and cyclic stability, as well as a good high-temperature tolerance. Particularly, it not only exhibited a high specific capacity of 135.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> but also a discharge capacity of 81.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with a high retention of 93.3% at the high current density of 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 2000 cycles. Moreover, when under harsh conditions, it enables the delivery of high specific capacities of 129.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> and 60 °C and 159.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> and 80 °C, respectively. Such a methodology also fits other K<sup>+</sup>, Zn<sup>2+</sup>, and La<sup>3+</sup> regulated materials design, thus implying its universal potential. Indeed, this work offers a new research perspective to improve the electrochemical performance of layered sodium oxides and SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Materials Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Na<sub>0.7</sub>MnO<sub>2.05</sub> was prepared by using a traditional solid-state synthesis method. Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (≥99.5%, Aladdin) and MnO<sub>2</sub> (≥99.5%, Aladdin) were uniformly ball-milled at 400 rpm for 12 h. The resulting mixture was pressed into sheets under of 6 MPa and then heated to 1000 °C at a rate of 5 °C per minute in an air atmosphere and held at 1000 °C for 15 h. The final product was naturally cooled to room temperature and stored in an argon-filled glovebox to prevent exposure to air. W-NMO was obtained by adding Na<sub>0.7</sub>MnO<sub>2.05</sub> to deionized water, stirring continuously at room temperature for 8 h, and then drying at 300 °C for 6 h. To synthesize disordered CNMO-1/CNMO-1.5, a mixture of 0.1/0.15 M CaCl<sub>2</sub> with DMF as the solvent was prepared. W-NMO was added to the prepared solution, followed by ultrasonic dispersion and continuous stirring at room temperature for 8 h for ion exchange. The collected powder was washed with DMF and dried at 300 °C for 6 h.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Physical Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Surface morphologies and elemental distributions were analyzed using field emission scanning electron microscopy (FE-SEM) with a JEOL JSM-7800F microscope, equipped with an energy-dispersive X-ray spectrometer (Ametek, TEAMOctane Plus), operated at an accelerating voltage of 15 kV. TEM was conducted on a JEM-2100 microscope with an X-Max80 detector operating at 200 kV to capture high-resolution images and diffraction patterns. XRD patterns were collected using a PANalytical Empyrean diffractometer, employing Cu Kα radiation (λ = 1.54065 Å) with a generator set to 45 kV and 40 mA. Raman spectra were obtained with a Thermo Fisher Renishaw inVia spectrometer using an excitation wavelength of 532 nm. XPS was performed on a Thermo Fisher Scientific K-Alpha spectrometer utilizing Al Kα radiation, with a scanning rate of 0.05 eV per step to determine the chemical states and composition. TGA was carried out using an STA 449 F5 analyzer (NETZSCH) to assess the thermal stability of the samples. FTIR spectra were recorded using a Shimadzu IR Affinity-1.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The prepared active materials were mixed with Super P carbon and poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF) binder in a 7:2:1 weight ratio using <i>N</i>-methyl pyrrolidone (NMP) as the solvent. The resulting slurry was cast onto aluminum foil and dried in a vacuum at 60 °C for 12 h. The typical loading mass of the active material was 0.7–1 mg/cm<sup>–2</sup>. Coin cells (CR2032) were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox (O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O &lt; 0.1 ppm), with sodium metal as the counter electrode and Whatman GF-A glass fiber as the separator. The electrolyte consisted of 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in dimethoxyethane (DME) with 2 wt % fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) as an additive. Electrochemical performance, including magnification characteristics and cycle stability, was tested using a LAND CT3001A battery test system at 25 °C within a voltage range of 2.0–4.0 V. CV curves were recorded at various scan rates using a Solartron Analytical 1470E station (Ametek) under the same voltage window.</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> Theoretical Calculations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">All the calculations are implemented by the VASP code. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> The GGA-PBE functional is selected for the exchange and correlation potential. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> Weak van der Waals interaction is considered by the DFT-D3 functional. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> The cutoff energy for the plane-wave is 400 eV. The k-mesh of 4 × 4 × 1 grid in the Brillouin-zone is chosen for integration. Total energies of the systems converge to 10<sup>–5</sup> eV in the iteration solution of the Kohn–Sham equation. The force on each atom reduced to 0.02 eV/Å after geometry optimization.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Material characterizations; electrochemical measurements; theoretical calculations; additional XRD patterns; SEM images; STEM images; TG curves; Raman images; XPS spectra; ICP-AES, and additional analysis of the electrochemical performance within a voltage range of 2–4 V (including the cycling performance, CV curves, EIS spectra etc.) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf">nn4c17035_si_001.pdf (2.76 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.4c17035" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent Advances in Sodium-Ion Battery Materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fang, Yongjin; Xiao, Lifen; Chen, Zhongxue; Ai, Xinping; Cao, Yuliang; Yang, Hanxi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochemical Energy Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">294-323</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EERLAM</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2520-8489</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer International Publishing AG</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Grid-scale energy storage systems with low-cost and high-performance electrodes are needed to meet the requirements of sustainable energy systems.  Due to the wide abundance and low cost of sodium resources and their similar electrochem. to the established lithium-ion batteries, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted considerable interest as ideal candidates for grid-scale energy storage systems.  In the past decade, though tremendous efforts have been made to promote the development of SIBs, and significant advances have been achieved, further improvements are still required in terms of energy/power d. and long cyclic stability for commercialization.  In this review, the latest progress in electrode materials for SIBs, including a variety of promising cathodes and anodes, is briefly summarized.  Besides, the sodium storage mechanisms, endeavors on electrochem. property enhancements, structural and compositional optimizations, challenges and perspectives of the electrode materials for SIBs are discussed.  Though enormous challenges may lie ahead, we believe that through intensive research efforts, sodium-ion batteries with low operation cost and longevity will be commercialized for large-scale energy storage application in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202202636"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Baumann, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Häringer, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schmidt, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schneider, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peters, J.
