        </div><div class="casAbstract">One approach to increasing the capacity of Li-ion batteries is to expand the usable voltage range over which the battery is cycled by charging the cathode to higher voltages.  Layered intercalation compds. commonly used as cathodes in Li-ion batteries, however, can become susceptible to irreversible structural changes at high states of charge due to cation migration to the emptied Li layers.  Here the discovery is reported of a strong correlation between the position of the Fermi level and the energy barrier for cation migration to the Li layers of fully charged layered intercalation compds.  Since the identity of the transition metal strongly affects the Fermi level, this discovery suggests that cation migration can be suppressed at high states of charge using targeted alloying strategies.  First-principles calcns. indicate that an increase in the concn. of Ni relative to Co or Mn should reduce, or even impede, interlayer migration in layered oxides.  The insights of this study pave the way for the discovery of layered intercalation compds. that can approach their theor. capacities in practice.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref51"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref51" class="refNumLink">51</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit51" data-doi="10.1016/j.joule.2022.05.005"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, C. Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, J. Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The timescale identification decoupling complicated kinetic processes in lithium batteries</span>. <i>Joule</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>6</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1172</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1198</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.joule.2022.05.005</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D6%26publication_year%3D2022%26pages%3D1172-1198%26journal%3DJoule%26author%3DY.%2BLu%26author%3DC.%2BZ.%2BZhao%26author%3DJ.%2BQ.%2BHuang%26author%3DQ.%2BZhang%26title%3DThe%2Btimescale%2Bidentification%2Bdecoupling%2Bcomplicated%2Bkinetic%2Bprocesses%2Bin%2Blithium%2Bbatteries%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.joule.2022.05.005&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.4c17035&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.joule.2022.05.005&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit51R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">51</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The timescale identification decoupling complicated kinetic processes in lithium batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lu, Yang; Zhao, Chen-Zi; Huang, Jia-Qi; Zhang, Qiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Joule</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1172-1198</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JOULBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2542-4351</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Cell Press</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A comprehensive understanding of multiple Li kinetics in batteries is essential to break the limitations of mechanism study and materials design.  Various kinetic processes with specific relaxation features can be clearly identified in timescales.  Extg. and analyzing the timescale information in batteries will provide insights into investigating kinetic issues such as ionic conductions, charge transfer, diffusions, interfacial evolutions, and other unknown kinetic processes.  In this regard, the timescale identification is an important method to combine with the non-destructive impedance characterizations in length scale for online battery monitoring.  This perspective introduces and advocates the timescale characterization in the views of the basic timescale property in batteries, employing the concept of distribution of relaxation time (DRT) and presenting successful applications for battery diagnosis.  In the future, we suggest that timescale characterizations will become powerful tools for data extn. and dataset building for various battery systems, which can realize data-driven machine learning modeling for practical application situations such as retired battery rapid sorting and battery status estns.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref52"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref52" class="refNumLink">52</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit52" data-doi="10.1149/2.0201514jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Clément, R. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bruce, P. G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Grey, C. P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Review─Manganese-Based P2-Type Transition Metal Oxides as Sodium-Ion Battery Cathode Materials</span>. <i>J. Electrochem. Soc.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>162</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">A2589</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">A2604</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1149/2.0201514jes</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D162%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3DA2589-A2604%26journal%3DJ.%2BElectrochem.%2BSoc.%26author%3DR.%2BJ.%2BCl%25C3%25A9ment%26author%3DP.%2BG.%2BBruce%26author%3DC.%2BP.%2BGrey%26title%3DReview%25E2%2594%2580Manganese-Based%2BP2-Type%2BTransition%2BMetal%2BOxides%2Bas%2BSodium-Ion%2BBattery%2BCathode%2BMaterials%26doi%3D10.1149%252F2.0201514jes&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.4c17035&amp;doiOfLink=10.1149%2F2.0201514jes&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit52R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">52</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Review-Manganese-Based P2-Type Transition Metal Oxides as Sodium-Ion Battery Cathode Materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Clement, Raphaele J.; Bruce, Peter G.; Grey, Clare P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">162</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">14</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">A2589-A2604</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JESOAN</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4651</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium transition metal oxides (NaMO2) with a P2 structure exhibit good Na+ ion cond. and are promising sodium-ion battery cathode materials.  Manganese-based compds. have a high working potential vs. Na+/Na, and high capacity.  Yet, the layered nature of these materials means that they are prone to structural rearrangements at high voltage/low Na contents, the phase transformations and Na+ ion/vacancy ordering transitions resulting in capacity fade and poor reversibility.  This review discusses the latest advances in the field and focuses mainly on recent work on NayMn1-xMxO2 (x, y ≤ 1, M = Ni, Mg, Li) compds.  We compare the different lithium and sodium transition metal layered oxides (P2, O3, etc.) and describe the structures and mechanisms obsd. on alkali (de)intercalation.  The strategies used to enhance the electrochem. properties and stabilize the structural framework of sodium transition metal oxides are reviewed.  We show how X-ray diffraction and 7Li/23Na solid-state NMR can be combined to provide a detailed insight into the structural and electronic processes occurring upon electrochem. cycling of these materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref53" class="refNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit53" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevB.54.11169"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kresse, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Furthmüller, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Efficient iterative schemes for <i>ab initio</i> total-energy calculations using a plane-wave basis set</span>. <i>Phys. Rev. B</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1996</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>54</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">11169</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">11186</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1103/PhysRevB.54.11169</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D54%26publication_year%3D1996%26pages%3D11169-11186%26journal%3DPhys.%2BRev.%2BB%26author%3DG.%2BKresse%26author%3DJ.%2BFurthm%25C3%25BCller%26title%3DEfficient%2Biterative%2Bschemes%2Bfor%2Bab%2Binitio%2Btotal-energy%2Bcalculations%2Busing%2Ba%2Bplane-wave%2Bbasis%2Bset%26doi%3D10.1103%252FPhysRevB.54.11169&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.4c17035&amp;doiOfLink=10.1103%2FPhysRevB.54.11169&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Efficient iterative schemes for ab initio total-energy calculations using a plane-wave basis set</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kresse, G.; Furthmueller, J.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Review B: Condensed Matter</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1996</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">54</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">16</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">11169-11186</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">PRBMDO</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0163-1829</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Physical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors present an efficient scheme for calcg. the Kohn-Sham ground state of metallic systems using pseudopotentials and a plane-wave basis set.  In the first part the application of Pulay's DIIS method (direct inversion in the iterative subspace) to the iterative diagonalization of large matrixes will be discussed.  This approach is stable, reliable, and minimizes the no. of order Natoms3 operations.  In the second part, we will discuss an efficient mixing scheme also based on Pulay's scheme.  A special "metric" and a special "preconditioning" optimized for a plane-wave basis set will be introduced.  Scaling of the method will be discussed in detail for non-self-consistent and self-consistent calcns.  It will be shown that the no. of iterations required to obtain a specific precision is almost independent of the system size.  Altogether an order Natoms2 scaling is found for systems contg. up to 1000 electrons.  If we take into account that the no. of k points can be decreased linearly with the system size, the overall scaling can approach Natoms.  They have implemented these algorithms within a powerful package called VASP (Vienna ab initio simulation package).  The program and the techniques have been used successfully for a large no. of different systems (liq. and amorphous semiconductors, liq. simple and transition metals, metallic and semiconducting surfaces, phonons in simple metals, transition metals, and semiconductors) and turned out to be very reliable.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref54" class="refNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit54" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevLett.77.3865"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Perdew, J. P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Burke, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ernzerhof, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Generalized Gradient Approximation Made Simple</span>. <i>Phys. Rev. Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1996</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>77</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3865</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3868</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1103/PhysRevLett.77.3865</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D77%26publication_year%3D1996%26pages%3D3865-3868%26journal%3DPhys.%2BRev.%2BLett.%26author%3DJ.%2BP.%2BPerdew%26author%3DK.%2BBurke%26author%3DM.%2BErnzerhof%26title%3DGeneralized%2BGradient%2BApproximation%2BMade%2BSimple%26doi%3D10.1103%252FPhysRevLett.77.3865&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.4c17035&amp;doiOfLink=10.1103%2FPhysRevLett.77.3865&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit54R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">54</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Generalized gradient approximation made simple</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Perdew, John P.; Burke, Kieron; Ernzerhof, Matthias</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Review Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1996</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">77</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">18</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3865-3868</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">PRLTAO</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0031-9007</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Physical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Generalized gradient approxns. (GGA's) for the exchange-correlation energy improve upon the local spin d. (LSD) description of atoms, mols., and solids.  We present a simple derivation of a simple GGA, in which all parameters (other than those in LSD) are fundamental consts.  Only general features of the detailed construction underlying the Perdew-Wang 1991 (PW91) GGA are invoked.  Improvements over PW91 include an accurate description of the linear response of the uniform electron gas, correct behavior under uniform scaling, and a smoother potential.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref55"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref55" class="refNumLink">55</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit55" data-doi="10.1063/1.3382344"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Grimme, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Antony, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ehrlich, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Krieg, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A consistent and accurate <i>ab initio</i> parametrization of density functional dispersion correction (DFT-D) for the 94 elements H-Pu</span>. <i>J. Chem. Phys.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2010</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>132</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">154104</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1063/1.3382344</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D132%26publication_year%3D2010%26pages%3D154104%26journal%3DJ.%2BChem.%2BPhys.%26author%3DS.%2BGrimme%26author%3DJ.%2BAntony%26author%3DS.%2BEhrlich%26author%3DH.%2BKrieg%26title%3DA%2Bconsistent%2Band%2Baccurate%2Bab%2Binitio%2Bparametrization%2Bof%2Bdensity%2Bfunctional%2Bdispersion%2Bcorrection%2B%2528DFT-D%2529%2Bfor%2Bthe%2B94%2Belements%2BH-Pu%26doi%3D10.1063%252F1.3382344&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.4c17035&amp;doiOfLink=10.1063%2F1.3382344&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A consistent and accurate ab initio parametrization of density functional dispersion correction (DFT-D) for the 94 elements H-Pu</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Grimme, Stefan; Antony, Jens; Ehrlich, Stephan; Krieg, Helge</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Chemical Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">132</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">15</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">154104/1-154104/19</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JCPSA6</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0021-9606</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Institute of Physics</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The method of dispersion correction as an add-on to std. Kohn-Sham d. functional theory (DFT-D) has been refined regarding higher accuracy, broader range of applicability, and less empiricism.  The main new ingredients are atom-pairwise specific dispersion coeffs. and cutoff radii that are both computed from first principles.  The coeffs. for new eighth-order dispersion terms are computed using established recursion relations.  System (geometry) dependent information is used for the first time in a DFT-D type approach by employing the new concept of fractional coordination nos. (CN).  They are used to interpolate between dispersion coeffs. of atoms in different chem. environments.  The method only requires adjustment of two global parameters for each d. functional, is asymptotically exact for a gas of weakly interacting neutral atoms, and easily allows the computation of at. forces.  Three-body nonadditivity terms are considered.  The method has been assessed on std. benchmark sets for inter- and intramol. noncovalent interactions with a particular emphasis on a consistent description of light and heavy element systems.  The mean abs. deviations for the S22 benchmark set of noncovalent interactions for 11 std. d. functionals decrease by 15%-40% compared to the previous (already accurate) DFT-D version.  Spectacular improvements are found for a tripeptide-folding model and all tested metallic systems.  The rectification of the long-range behavior and the use of more accurate C6 coeffs. also lead to a much better description of large (infinite) systems as shown for graphene sheets and the adsorption of benzene on an Ag(111) surface.  For graphene it is found that the inclusion of three-body terms substantially (by about 10%) weakens the interlayer binding.  We propose the revised DFT-D method as a general tool for the computation of the dispersion energy in mols. and solids of any kind with DFT and related (low-cost) electronic structure methods for large systems.  (c) 2010 American Institute of Physics.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" Structural characteristics. (a) XRD patterns, (b) enlarged XRD patterns, (c) Rietveld refined XRD pattern with the corresponding cell parameters of the as-synthesized disordered CNMO-1 material, and (d) schematic crystal structure of disordered CNMO-1. (e) HAADF-STEM image of CNMO-1 and the corresponding (f) intensity line profiles obtained from the area marked by white rectangles, and (g) SEM, (h) HRTEM, and (i) Raman spectra. (j) FTIR spectra of pristine NMO and CNMO-1 after being exposed in moist air. Calculation model and the corresponding adsorption energy of Ca (k) and Na (l) to water in NCMO.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.4c17035&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig3"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/medium/nn4c17035_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0003.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr3" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical performance test. (a) Rate capability, (b) long-term cycle performance at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 10 cycles of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> activation and (c) at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Comparison between this work and the previously reported layered oxides as cathodes for SIBs at different current densities. High-temperature performance test of the disordered CNMO-1 and ordered NMO cathodes. (e) Cycle performance at 60 °C and 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 5 cycles of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> and (f) cycle performance at 80 °C and 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 5 cycles of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.4c17035&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig4"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/medium/nn4c17035_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0004.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr4" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (b) GITT curves and the calculated diffusion coefficient values. (c) Ex situ Raman spectra of disordered CNMO-1. (d) Schematic diagram of the process of Na<sup>+</sup> rearrangement. (e) DRT evolution of the in situ impedance of disordered CNMO-1.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.4c17035&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig5"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/medium/nn4c17035_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0005.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr5" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Study on the structural and morphological evolution of ordered NMO and disordered CNMO-1 electrodes upon cycling. Voltage profiles and contour plots of in situ XRD patterns for (a) CNMO-1 and (b) NMO. HRTEM images were recorded in the fifth cycle and charged to 4 V: (c) CNMO-1 and (d) NMO. Partial enlarged HRTEM images of (e) CNMO-1 and (f) NMO. Ex situ FESEM images of (g) CNMO-1 and (h) NMO electrodes after 100 cycles at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.4c17035&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig6"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/medium/nn4c17035_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0006.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr6" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Theoretical investigations on the reaction thermodynamics of electrodes. (a) Simulated the phase formation energy changes of ordered NMO at various desodiation states for both the P2 and OP4 phases. Corresponding crystal structures of (b) P2 and (c) OP4 under different desodiation states. (d) Simulated the phase formation energy changes of CNMO-1 for the P2 and OP4 phases across different Ca content states. Corresponding crystal structures of (e) P2 and (f) OP4 under different Ca content states.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.4c17035&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig7"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/medium/nn4c17035_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/asset/images/large/nn4c17035_0007.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr7" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7.         ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2520-8489</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer International Publishing AG</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Grid-scale energy storage systems with low-cost and high-performance electrodes are needed to meet the requirements of sustainable energy systems.  Due to the wide abundance and low cost of sodium resources and their similar electrochem. to the established lithium-ion batteries, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted considerable interest as ideal candidates for grid-scale energy storage systems.  In the past decade, though tremendous efforts have been made to promote the development of SIBs, and significant advances have been achieved, further improvements are still required in terms of energy/power d. and long cyclic stability for commercialization.  In this review, the latest progress in electrode materials for SIBs, including a variety of promising cathodes and anodes, is briefly summarized.  Besides, the sodium storage mechanisms, endeavors on electrochem. property enhancements, structural and compositional optimizations, challenges and perspectives of the electrode materials for SIBs are discussed.  Though enormous challenges may lie ahead, we believe that through intensive research efforts, sodium-ion batteries with low operation cost and longevity will be commercialized for large-scale energy storage application in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202202636"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Baumann, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Häringer, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schmidt, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schneider, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peters, J. F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bauer, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Binder, J. R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Weil, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Prospective Sustainability Screening of Sodium-Ion Battery Cathode Materials</span>. <i>Adv. Energy Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>12</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2202636</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/aenm.202202636</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="

                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1021/acs.accounts.2c00690"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zuo, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Innocenti, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zarrabeitia, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bresser, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Passerini, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Layered Oxide Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Storage Mechanism, Electrochemistry, and Techno-economics</span>. <i>Acc. Chem. Res.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2023</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>56</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">284</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">296</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.accounts.2c00690</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Layered Oxide Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Storage Mechanism, Electrochemistry, and Techno-economics</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zuo, Wenhua; Innocenti, Alessandro; Zarrabeitia, Maider; Bresser, Dominic; Yang, Yong; Passerini, Stefano</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Accounts of Chemical Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2023</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">56</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">284-296</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACHRE4</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0001-4842</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Conspectus: Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) are ubiquitous in all modern portable electronic devices such as mobile phones and laptops as well as for powering hybrid elec. vehicles (EVs) and other large-scale devices.  Sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) which possess a similar cell configuration and working mechanism have already been proven as ideal alternatives for large-scale energy storage systems.  The advantages of NIBs are as follows.  First, sodium resources are abundantly distributed in the earth's crust.  Second, high-performance NIB cathode materials can be fabricated by using solely inexpensive and noncrit. transition metals such as manganese (Mn) and iron (Fe), which further reduces the cost of the required raw materials.  Recently, the unprecedented demand for lithium and other crit. minerals has driven the cost of these primary raw materials (which are utilized in LIBs) to a historic high and thus triggered the commercialization of NIBs.  Sodium layered transition metal oxides (NaxTMO2, TM = transition metal/s), such as Mn-based sodium layered oxides, represent an important family of cathode materials with the potential to reduce costs, increase energy d. and cycling stability, along with improving the safety of NIBs for large-scale energy storage.  However, these layered oxides face several key challenges, including irreversible phase transformations during cycling, poor air-stability, complex charge-compensation mechanisms, and relatively high cost of the full cell compared to LiFePO4-based LIBs.  Our work has focused on the techno-economic anal., the degrdn. mechanism of NaxTMO2 upon cycling and air exposure, and the development of effective strategies to improve their electrochem. performances and air stability.  Correlating structure-performance relationships and establishing general design strategies of NaxTMO2 must be considered for the commercialization of NIBs.  In this account, we discuss the recent progress in the development of air-stable, electrochem. stable, and cost-effective NaxTMO2.  The favorable redox active cations for NaxTMO2 are emphasized in terms of abundance, cost, supply, and energy d.  Different working mechanisms related to NaxTMO2 are summarized, including the electrochem. reversibility, the main structural transformations during the charge and discharge processes, and the charge-compensation mechanisms that accompany the (de)intercalation of Na+ ions, followed by discussions to improve the stability toward ambient air and upon cycling.  Then, the techno-economics are presented with an emphasis on cathodes with different chem. compns., cost breakdown of battery packs, and Na deficiency; factors that are crit. to the large-scale implementation.  Finally, this account concludes with an overview of the remaining challenges and new opportunities concerning the practical applications of NaxTMO2 with an emphasis on the cost, large-scale fabrication capability, and electrochem. performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.54227/elab.20230008"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Emerging presodiation strategies for long-life sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Energy Lab</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2023</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>1</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">230008</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                10.1016/j.nanoen.2022.106958</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Triggering anionic redox activity in Fe/Mn-based layered oxide for high-performance sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Ziwei; Yang, Maolin; Chen, Guojie; Tang, Guangxia; Huang, Zhongyuan; Chu, Mihai; Qi, Rui; Li, Simo; Wang, Rui; Wang, Chaoqi; Zhang, Taolve; Zhai, Jingjun; Zhao, Wenguang; Zhang, Junrong; Chen, Jie; He, Lunhua; Xu, Juping; Yin, Wen; Wang, Jun; Xiao, Yinguo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">94</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">106958</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANCA</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2211-2855</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Among various sodium-ion cathode materials, Fe/Mn-based layered oxides stand out due to cost-effectiveness and relatively high theor. specific capacity.  However, further enhancement in capacity and improvement in cyclability are still needed to meet the requirements for practical applications in sodium-ion batteries.  Herein, we report that ruthenium-doped Na0.67Fe0.5Mn0.5O2 can not only achieve high reversible capacity but also deliver superior cycling stability.  Owing to the substitution of 4d ruthenium heteroatoms, the cathode exhibits promising electrochem. performance with a higher reversible specific capacity of 170 mA h g-1 at 0.2C between 2 and 4 V as well as a stable cycling performance with 82.2% capacity retention at 2C after 100 cycles compared to its pristine counterpart.  Advanced structural characterization techniques combined with theor. calcns. unveil that the presence of ruthenium ions can trigger anionic redox activity, thus leading to harvesting of extra capacity.  Moreover, ruthenium ions can also play an important role in stabilizing and improving the structural framework, resulting in prominent cyclability and excellent rate performance.  Overall, the present work demonstrates that anionic redox activity could be triggered by integration of trace 4d element in Fe/Mn-based layered oxides and represents an effective strategy to develop high-performance sodium cathodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref13" class="rightTabRefNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit13" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.2c09725"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ren, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhong, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hua, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Indris, S.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Homeostatic Solid Solution in Layered Transition-Metal Oxide Cathodes of Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Two-phase transformation reaction is ubiquitous in solid-state electrochem.; however, it usually involves inferior structure rearrangement upon extn. and insertion of large-sized Na+, thus leading to severe local strain, cracks, and capacity decay in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  Here, a homeostatic solid soln. reaction is reported in the layered cathode material P'2-Na0.653Ni0.081Mn0.799Ti0.120O2 during sodiation and desodiation.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered transition metal oxide P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 usually suffers from large-vol. phase transitions and different Na-vacancy ordering during sodium (de)intercalation, incurring rapid capacity decline and poor rate capability.  Herein, an effective strategy based on synergetic effect of selected multiple metal ions is designed for P2-type cathodes with improved performance.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries have garnered unprecedented attention as an electrochem. energy storage technol., but it remains challenging to design high-energy-d. cathode materials with low structural strain during the dynamic (de)sodiation processes.  Herein, we report a P2-layered lithium dual-site-substituted Na0.7Li0.03[Mg0.15Li0.07Mn0.75]O2 (NMLMO) cathode material, in which Li ions occupy both transition-metal (TM) and alkali-metal (AM) sites. In this study, an ultrahigh reversible capacity is demonstrated by a layered oxide cathode purely based on manganese.  Through a wealth of characterizations, it is clarified that the presence of low-content Li2MnO3 domains not only reduces the amt. of irreversible O loss; but also regulates Mn migration in LiMnO2 domains, enabling elastic lattice with high reversibility for tetrahedral sites Li-ion storage in Li layers.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Crystals are a state of matter characterized by periodic order.  Yet, cryst. materials can harbor disorder in many guises, such as non-repeating variations in compn., atom displacements, bonding arrangements, mol. orientations, conformations, charge states, orbital occupancies or magnetic structure.  Disorder can sometimes be random but, more usually, it is correlated.  Frontier research into disordered crystals now seeks to control and exploit the unusual patterns that persist within these correlated disordered states in order to access functional responses inaccessible to conventional crystals.  In this Review, we survey the core design principles that guide targeted control over correlated disorder.  We show how these principles - often informed by long-studied statistical mech. models - can be applied across an unexpectedly broad range of materials, including orgs., supramol. assemblies, oxide ceramics and metal-org. frameworks. Investigation of the organization of interlayer water and cations in smectite is a permanent topic in clay science for environmental science, civil engineering, materials science, and industrial applications.  Exptl. X-ray (or neutron) diffraction methods and mol. simulations are key techniques to probe the organization of the smectite structure at a similar mol. length scale.  The combination of both of these exptl. and numerical methods represents a complementary approach to reveal the structural heterogeneity of real samples, design and model a wide range of smectite structures, and validate the simulation results through comparison with exptl. data. This calcn. toolbox is also used to det. better the layer-to-layer distances considered in mol. simulations and to validate these simulations through a detailed collation procedure using exptl. data.  Recent examples of the application of such a procedure to collate exptl. diffraction data and mol. simulations are presented for the specific case of deciphering the mol. organization of interlayer water and cations in the different smectite hydrates (mono-, bi-, and tri-hydrated layers). The promising P2-layered sodium transition metal oxides (P2-NaxTmO2) often suffer from structural/chem. transformations when contacted with moist air.  However, these elaborate transitions and the evaluation rules towards air-stable P2-NaxTmO2 have not yet been clearly elucidated.  Herein, taking P2-Na0.67MnO2 and P2-Na0.67Ni0.33Mn0.67O2 as key examples, we unveil the comprehensive structural/chem. degrdn. mechanisms of P2-NaxTmO2 in different ambient atmospheres by using various microscopic/spectroscopic characterizations and first-principle calcns.  The extent of bulk structural/chem. transformation of P2-NaxTmO2 is detd. by the amt. of extd. Na+, which is mainly compensated by Na+/H+ exchange.  By expanding our study to a series of Mn-based oxides, we reveal that the air-stability of P2-NaxTmO2 is highly related to their oxidn. features in the first charge process and further propose a practical evaluating rule assocd. with redox couples for air-stable NaxTmO2 cathodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref36" class="rightTabRefNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit36" data-doi="10.1016/j.matlet.2014.07.153"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kirie, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tanaka, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Unno, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamamoto, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Noguchi, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis of metal ion substituted P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode material with enhanced performance for Na ion batteries</span>. <i>Mater.         </div><div class="casAbstract">O3-Type layered oxides are widely studied as cathodes for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to their high theor. capacities.  However, their rate capability and durability are limited by tortuous Na+ diffusion channels and complicated phase evolution during Na+ extn./insertion.  Here we report our findings in unravelling the mechanism for dramatically enhancing the stability and rate capability of O3-NaNi0.5Mn0.5-xSbxO2 (NaNMS) by substitutional Sb doping, which can alter the coordination environment and chem. bonds of the transition metal (TM) ions in the structure, resulting in a more stable structure with wider Na+ transport channels.  Furthermore, NaNMS nanoparticles are obtained by surface energy regulation during grain growth.  The synergistic effect of Sb doping and nanostructuring greatly reduces the ionic migration energy barrier while increasing the reversibility of the structural evolution during repeated Na+ extn./insertion.  An optimized NaNMS-1 electrode delivers a reversible capacity of 212.3 mAh g-1 at 0.2 C and 74.5 mAh g-1 at 50 C with minimal capacity loss after 100 cycles at a low temp. of -20°C.  Such electrochem. performance is superior to most of the reported layered oxide cathodes used in rechargeable SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref41" class="rightTabRefNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit41" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2022.138912"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ouyang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Construction of Co/Ni-Free P2-layered metal oxide cathode with high reversible oxygen redox for sodium ion batteries</span>. <i>Chem. E.; Birss, V.; Wojtowicz, J.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1997</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">66</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1-2</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The principle of utilizing the non-Faradaic double-layer capacitance of electrode interfaces as a means of storing elec. energy was suggested and utilized in technologies initiated some 37 yr ago.  However, only over the last ten years has major interest been manifested in com. development of this possibility in so-called 'supercapacitors' or 'ultracapacitors' based on the large double-layer capacitance achievable at high-area, carbon powder electrodes.  In parallel with the utilization of double-layer capacitance is the possibility of use of the large pseudocapacitance that is assocd. with e.g. electrosorption of H or metal adatoms (underpotential deposition) and esp. some redox processes.  Such pseudocapacitance arises when, for thermodn. reasons, the charge q required for progression of an electrode process, e.g. electrosorption or conversion of an oxidized species to a corresponding reduced species in liq. or solid soln., is a continuous function of potential, V; then the deriv. dq/dV corresponds to a capacitance but one of a Faradaic kind.  This behavior is different from that with an ideal battery where, according to the Nernst equation, V is invariant with state-of-charge measured by q.  Various exptl. examples are shown and characterized, esp. that for RuO2 and other transition metal oxides.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Cation migration often occurs in layered oxide cathodes of lithium-ion batteries due to the similar ion radius of Li and transition metals (TMs).                 10.1063/1.3382344</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A consistent and accurate ab initio parametrization of density functional dispersion correction (DFT-D) for the 94 elements H-Pu</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Grimme, Stefan; Antony, Jens; Ehrlich, Stephan; Krieg, Helge</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Chemical Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">132</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">15</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">154104/1-154104/19</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JCPSA6</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0021-9606</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Institute of Physics</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The method of dispersion correction as an add-on to std. Kohn-Sham d. functional theory (DFT-D) has been refined regarding higher accuracy, broader range of applicability, and less empiricism.  The main new ingredients are atom-pairwise specific dispersion coeffs. and cutoff radii that are both computed from first principles.  The coeffs. for new eighth-order dispersion terms are computed using established recursion relations.  System (geometry) dependent information is used for the first time in a DFT-D type approach by employing the new concept of fractional coordination nos. (CN).  They are used to interpolate between dispersion coeffs. of atoms in different chem. environments.  The method only requires adjustment of two global parameters for each d. functional, is asymptotically exact for a gas of weakly interacting neutral atoms, and easily allows the computation of at. forces.  Three-body nonadditivity terms are considered.  The method has been assessed on std. benchmark sets for inter- and intramol. noncovalent interactions with a particular emphasis on a consistent description of light and heavy element systems.  The mean abs. deviations for the S22 benchmark set of noncovalent interactions for 11 std. d. functionals decrease by 15%-40% compared to the previous (already accurate) DFT-D version.  Spectacular improvements are found for a tripeptide-folding model and all tested metallic systems.  The rectification of the long-range behavior and the use of more accurate C6 coeffs. also lead to a much better description of large (infinite) systems as shown for graphene sheets and the adsorption of benzene on an Ag(111) surface.  For graphene it is found that the inclusion of three-body terms substantially (by about 10%) weakens the interlayer binding.  We propose the revised DFT-D method as a general tool for the computation of the dispersion energy in mols. and solids of any kind with DFT and related (low-cost) electronic structure methods for large systems.  (c) 2010 American Institute of Physics.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i12">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Material characterizations; electrochemical measurements; theoretical calculations; additional XRD patterns; SEM images; STEM images; TG curves; Raman images; XPS spectra; ICP-AES, and additional analysis of the electrochemical performance within a voltage range of 2–4 V (including the cycling performance, CV curves, EIS spectra etc.) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.4c17035/suppl_file/nn4c17035_si_001.pdf">nn4c17035_si_001.pdf (2.76 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

        <script nonce="93188d1ddb41075e-SJC">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

<script nonce="93188d1ddb41075e-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>

<script nonce="93188d1ddb41075e-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      function init() {
        setTimeout(function () {
          var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
            return false;
          } else {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
            gdprMessage();
          }
          //Events
          $("#closebtn").on("click", hideForm);

          //Handle submit
          $("#gdpr-message-btn").on("click", function (event) {
            handleClick();

      function gdprErrorMessage() {

      function validateEmail(email) {
        var re =
          /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

  </script>

        <script nonce="93188d1ddb41075e-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Nano";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>

    var uri = "/doi/full/10.1021/html_original_uri?id=123";
    console.log("ACSPubs2 start");
    $.getJSON('https://extreme-ip-lookup.com/json/', function(data, status) {
            if ( status == 'success' ) {
                $.each(data, function(i, field){
                    cookie=cookie.concat(i+' = '+field+" - ");
                });
                
            } else {
                    cookie=status;
            }

            cookie1 = cookie;
            cookie = cookie1 + "; path=/; domain=.acs.org";
            //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
            $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
            //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
            console.log("ACSPubs2 success");
    })
    .fail(function(xx, status, err){
        cookie="error: 127.0.0.1";
        //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
        $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
        console.log("ACSPubs2 failed");
        //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
    });
});    
</script>
